  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c3c00031-932f-4d8c-85d5-202e7a27fb73'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer11689730719114891097">R.U.R.--written in 1920--garnered worldwide acclaim for its author and popularized the word "Robot." Mass-produced, efficient and servile labor, Čapek's Robots remember everything, but lack creative thought, and the Utopian life they provide ultimately lacks meaning. When the Robots revolt, killing all but one of their masters, they must attempt to learn the secret of self</span>
  <span id="freeText11689730719114891097" style="display:none">R.U.R.--written in 1920--garnered worldwide acclaim for its author and popularized the word "Robot." Mass-produced, efficient and servile labor, Čapek's Robots remember everything, but lack creative thought, and the Utopian life they provide ultimately lacks meaning. When the Robots revolt, killing all but one of their masters, they must attempt to learn the secret of self-duplication. But their attempts at replication leave them with nothing but bloody chunks of meat. It's not until two robots fall in love and are christened "Adam" and "Eve" by the last surviving human that Nature emerges triumphant.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11689730719114891097" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="bookReviews">
  




  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1753451143" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1753451143" />
    <a title="Glenn Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell"><img alt="Glenn Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505195595p2/23385697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1753451143?book_show_action=true">Sep 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Russell" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell">Glenn Russell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182087" href="/book/show/816443.R_U_R_">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1753451143" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7471596967982256799"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1473554476i/20481357._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Here are ten philosophical insights embedded in the extended prologue to this highly inventive 1920 science fiction three-act play by Czechoslovakian author Karel Čapek. And, yes, this play marks the very first appearance of the term “Robot” as in R.U.R. – Rossum’s Universal Robots – mass produced, human-like machines to perform manual labor and function as servants. <br/><br/>1. Old man Rossum was a biologist who failed to create actual humans in his laboratory; engineer son Rossum invented the living la</span>
  <span id="freeText7471596967982256799" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1473554476i/20481357._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Here are ten philosophical insights embedded in the extended prologue to this highly inventive 1920 science fiction three-act play by Czechoslovakian author Karel Čapek. And, yes, this play marks the very first appearance of the term “Robot” as in R.U.R. – Rossum’s Universal Robots – mass produced, human-like machines to perform manual labor and function as servants. <br><br>1. Old man Rossum was a biologist who failed to create actual humans in his laboratory; engineer son Rossum invented the living labor machine, the Robot, a natural progression of production (son) following discovery (father). After all, as the present central director of R.U.R. states: “If you can’t do it faster than nature, what’s the point?” Let’s not forget this is 1920, the engineer is king and speed, machines, factories and efficiency are all the rage. Speed and machines even made headway in the world of art some year prior, especially among the Italian Futurists such as Mainetti, Boccioni and Bella.<br><br>2. The central director continues: “Production should be as simple as possible and the product the best for its function.” And “The creation of an engineer is technically more refined than the product of nature.” The spirit of these statements was captured magnificently in the film “Modern Times” with Charlie Chaplin. Since the prologue is peppered alternately with satire, comedy and black humor, it’s as if the creators of that Chaplin film mined a number of ideas from Čapek’s play.<br><br>3. Young Rossum started with twelve-foot Superrobots but they kept falling apart so he began manufacturing Robots of normal human height and respectable human shape. Curiously, when Robots mimic human dimensions, there is something inexplicably appealing about their physical presence. For example, witness the computer generated American football playing Robot one of the large commercial stations uses in their broadcasts – the Robot signals a first down, spikes the ball and even does a little dance after scoring a touchdown – all very charming for football fans – just Robot enough to be fantasy; just human enough to seemingly possess human emotions and feelings.<br><br>4. A recent arrival to the R.U.R. factory, Helena, a sensitive young lady converses with the central director and mistakes the director’s beautiful secretary, a Robot named Sulla, for a real person. Oh, no, no! Helena is quite upset and initially refuses to belief such a gorgeous woman, just like herself, isn’t human. Ah, there’s something so very compelling about a woman’s beauty – we refuse to believe a young woman with such beauty lacks heart and feeling – case in point, the 2015 film “Ex Machina.”<br><br>5. The way they are constructed, Robots never think up anything original. As the director notes: “They’d make fine university professors.” Did I mention Karel Čapek’s infusion of satire and black humor? Such a nice touch – not too much satire to sound preachy but enough to let us know much in his society and culture, all the flummery about “progress” and the eventual perfection and purification of mankind through scientism, is so much smoke and mirrors.<br><br>6. Turns out Helena has traveled to the R.U.R. factory on behalf of the League of Humanity in order to incite and liberate the Robots. All the factory directors in the room, all six men, laugh and tell Helena everybody from the outside who comes to the factory wants to do good by the Robots and set them free. As the central director, Harry Domin, informs Helena: “It would amaze you to know how many churches and lunatics there are in the world.” Churches and lunatics . . . you gotta love it! As events unfold beginning in Act One, Helena’s words take on stinging irony.<br><br>7. Helena wants to know if you can show the Robots a bit of love. Impossible, retorts the directors, since Robots are made for one and only one purpose: work. For Robots have no sense of taste nor do they ever smile. Considering how many 21st century factory Robots have been successfully constructed for nonstop work, this passage takes on a particular resonance. And, God forbid, if there is work where Robots can’t replace humans, there is always the opportunity to move your factory to a third world country and corral the poverty-stricken into your sweat-shop for next to nothing. Much of the themes of R.U.R. are as relevant today as they were in 1920.<br><br>8. But, but, but . . . there is a chink in the armor. As one director sadly states: “Occasionally they go crazy somehow. Something like epilepsy, you know? We call it Robotic Palsy. All of a sudden one of them goes and breaks whatever it has in its hands, stops working, gnashes its teeth – and we have to send it to the stamping mill. Evidently a breakdown of the organism.” Sound like trouble? Such breakdowns prove to be big trouble.<br><br>9. Powerful economic forces are at work. One of the directors pronounces how their Robots have cut the cost of labor, so much so that non-Robot factories are going belly-up. Many are the zingers the playwright hurls at a society reduced to the forces of supply and demand where the requirement to produce profits for shareholders takes top priority at the expense of humanity. Money, money, money . . . the lifeblood of the modern world then and now. <br><br>10. Harry Domin proclaims how no longer will mankind have to destroy his soul doing work he hates; people will live to perfect themselves. Considering the modern phenomenon of the couch potato and numerous other mind-numbing addictions, unfortunately there is substantial evidence the vast percentage of the population is far from “perfecting itself” given time free from work. <br><br>So, Act One, Act Two, Act Three take place at the R.U.R. factory ten years after the prologue. It's Robots vs. humans. I encourage you to read the play to find out what happens. And I highly recommend this Penguin edition which includes a most informative introductory essay by Czeck writer Ivan Klima.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1473721833i/20506408.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="7471596967982256799" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3319240607623560115">This is the book that introduced concept of robots. And the play seems to get a lot of things about dynamics involved right too, and surprisingly right. It is criticism of result and productivity centered approach that seems to have taken over the world ever since industrial revolution:<br/><br/>" From a technical point of view, the whole of childhood is quite pointless. Simply a waste of time."<br/><br/>Or<br/><br/>" He took a good look at the human body and he saw straight away that it was much too complicated, any good en</span>
  <span id="freeText3319240607623560115" style="display:none">This is the book that introduced concept of robots. And the play seems to get a lot of things about dynamics involved right too, and surprisingly right. It is criticism of result and productivity centered approach that seems to have taken over the world ever since industrial revolution:<br /><br />" From a technical point of view, the whole of childhood is quite pointless. Simply a waste of time."<br /><br />Or<br /><br />" He took a good look at the human body and he saw straight away that it was much too complicated, any good engineer would design it much more simply."<br /><br />Or <br /><br />"Man is a being that does things such as feeling happiness, plays the violin, likes to go for a walk, and all sorts of other things which are simply not needed."<br /><br />The people that are too sure of themselves to say things like that are such because they believe in one and only one value (in this case productivity, in Hitler's case it was imagined superiority of Aryan race). And thus they are wrong.<br /><br />The Best part for me though is Helena's reaction. We develop compassion to things or people in proportion of how much they are like us - their physical similarity seems to imply that they must have similar feelings too. We are more compassionate to humans than animals, to mammals than insects etc. And thus, Helena can't understand that a machine that looks so much like a human doesn't have emotions.<br /><br />Now, politicians know it best. That is why they must create or emphasize already existing artificial categories - race, caste, class, sex, rationality, religion etc. The R.U.R. knows it too and uses it to keep robots from getting united.<br /><br />"... each factory will produce robots of a different colour, different hair, different language. The robots will be strangers to each other, they'll never be able to understand what the other says; and we, we humans, we'll train them so that each robot will hate the robots from another factory all its life, all through to the grave, all through all eternity."<br /><br />Read Glenn's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1753451143?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener">review</a>.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer1659079131105257261">R.U.R, Rossum's Universal Robots. Written in 1920 by Czech writer Karel Capek. It is a science fiction play, and it has the distinction of introducing the word robot into the English language. Here comes a big spoiler; humans build robots to make their life better, robots become self aware and kill humans to make the world better. All the other species in the world gave a big thank you to the robots. That wasn't in the play, I just added that.</span>

<div id="review_2406802794" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2406802794" />
    <a title="Dan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5411470-dan"><img alt="Dan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587053101p2/5411470.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2406802794?book_show_action=true">May 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/5411470-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671466053" href="/book/show/3034409-r-u-r">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5411470-dan?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2406802794" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2062809022173799814">Finishing R.U.R. was a bit of a chore and gets three stars, mostly on the strength of the brief pieces of dialogue between the characters. I can see how that and that alone might work with an audience. No long soliloquies. But that is all I can appreciate about R.U.R. <br/><br/>The play itself is muddled in different genres, and makes a grandiose point in the tritest manner for its conclusion at the end of the third act. With the Epilogue, R.U.R. tries to arrive at a more meaningful conclusion than the th</span>
  <span id="freeText2062809022173799814" style="display:none">Finishing R.U.R. was a bit of a chore and gets three stars, mostly on the strength of the brief pieces of dialogue between the characters. I can see how that and that alone might work with an audience. No long soliloquies. But that is all I can appreciate about R.U.R. <br /><br />The play itself is muddled in different genres, and makes a grandiose point in the tritest manner for its conclusion at the end of the third act. With the Epilogue, R.U.R. tries to arrive at a more meaningful conclusion than the third act does. However, the Epilogue of R.U.R, has essentially the same ending as the re-imagined Battlestar Galactica (BG) TV series, both of which fail for the same reason--they can't justify what led up to it. <br /><br />Most critics call R.U.R. satire. If so, it is failed satire. It is too serious a treatment to make mankind's institutions appear ridiculous.<br /><br />If it is science fiction, it fails because it is not serious enough to justify the magnitude of its stakes. The very survival of mankind, undone because people stop procreating? Why? Or the survival of robots, undone because Helena burns a couple pieces of paper? How?<br /><br />I know R.U.R. preceded BG, but the two are so alike in concept that I can't really separate the two. I think one reason I find the play so utterly boring is because it spends 114 pages in my edition rendering what it took BG a few minutes to cover, most of that off screen. Namely, the origin of their conflict. It's just not a sufficiently interesting concept to justify hours long treatment. If it somehow could be, Capek didn't prove it here.<br /><br />Others might say, "Oh but there are such wonderful, intellectual ideas. Capek has so much to say about capitalism and societal roles." If so, I couldn't find a deep or unmuddled message in what I read. Workers are exploited. No kidding! Creating people to do the mundane tasks of society's labor may some day result in violent revolution. You don't say? Sorry, but these are socialist platitudes, not ideas.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2062809022173799814" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2357188765" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2357188765" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44737540-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601186771p2/44737540.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2357188765?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/44737540-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44737540-patrick?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44737540-patrick?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44737540-patrick?shelf=2018-reading-challenge">2018-reading-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2357188765" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer428382600338653323">R.U.R. (Rossum’s Universal Robots) by Karel Capek is an interesting read. It is a Sci-fi play. This story was completely new to me. I didn’t know who came up with robots but now I know. This is the first Sci-fi story with the word Robot. The world of Sci-fi can thank this man for bringing Robots into the world. So, Karel, thank you! <br/><br/>Old Rossum, a mad scientist, wanted to be a substitute for God and decided to make man, but years came and went and nothing happened. No life. Young Rossum decided h</span>
  <span id="freeText428382600338653323" style="display:none">R.U.R. (Rossum’s Universal Robots) by Karel Capek is an interesting read. It is a Sci-fi play. This story was completely new to me. I didn’t know who came up with robots but now I know. This is the first Sci-fi story with the word Robot. The world of Sci-fi can thank this man for bringing Robots into the world. So, Karel, thank you! <br /><br />Old Rossum, a mad scientist, wanted to be a substitute for God and decided to make man, but years came and went and nothing happened. No life. Young Rossum decided he would take on his father’s dream, and ditch his father’s crazy plan for making man and made a machine instead. Man-like but without a soul. He made a “skin” very much like man’s to make them look like a human being. Rossum’s dream is to create so much food and goods that it will be practically free and man will reign as the lords of creation. There won’t be war, poverty, and starvation…or so they say. The scientists discover, as they are making robots more and more into the image of man, they are destroying themselves and the rest of mankind. <br /><br />This play/novella was very thought provoking. What makes man, well…man? Is it his soul? Is it his morals, or lack there of? Is it emotion? Can man make man? God created man, and thus the soul, but can man make a soul? All these, and more, are answered in the story. What are all the components of mankind? <br /><br />If I were a high school teacher I would definitely read this book in class. It is very thought provoking and I can see talking with another person for hours on end about R.U.R. Great book! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="428382600338653323" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2392780186" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2392780186" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/695116-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340192527p2/695116.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2392780186?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/695116-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook " href="/book/show/25900396-r-u-r---rossum-s-universal-robots">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=3classics">3classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=3plays-poetry">3plays-poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=1audio">1audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/695116-jim?shelf=2fiction">2fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2392780186" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6741165033642155717">I'm not sure what to make of this. It's a classic of SF, the origin of the word 'robot' although the idea had been around for quite some time & the 'robot's are actually not mechanical, but biological androids more like those in "Blade Runner". The story itself oscillates wildly between extremely profound to awful. <br/><br/>On the profound side is the entire idea. Capek encapsulates all our technological striving into one, short play. Overall, it's fantastic. There are also some great quotes:<br/><i>A guilty par</i></span>
  <span id="freeText6741165033642155717" style="display:none">I'm not sure what to make of this. It's a classic of SF, the origin of the word 'robot' although the idea had been around for quite some time &amp; the 'robot's are actually not mechanical, but biological androids more like those in "Blade Runner". The story itself oscillates wildly between extremely profound to awful. <br /><br />On the profound side is the entire idea. Capek encapsulates all our technological striving into one, short play. Overall, it's fantastic. There are also some great quotes:<br /><i>A guilty party is being sought. Such action is a favorite means of consolation in the face of calamity.</i><br />More can be found here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/Karel_%C4%8Capek#R.U.R._(1920)">https://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/Karel_%...</a><br /><br />On the awful side, we have the action at the end of Act 1 <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ when Helena suddenly marries Domin. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> It made no sense to me at all. There was no lead in to it, just a weird leap that I had to swallow to continue on. Jarring &amp; ridiculous.<br /><br />There are also translation issues. This version melds some characters, but I doubt that harmed it much. The Librivox cast did a great job reading this. From the discussion in the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/19315813" rel="nofollow noopener">Evolution of SF topic</a> about this book, a lot of beautiful phrases &amp; allusions are lost in translation. We're lucky enough to have a someone who speaks the language &amp; has read it in its original form. Oleksandr is also quite fluent in English &amp; an SF fan, so has shed a lot of light on it. Thanks, Oleksandr!<br /><br />This is a play &amp; would probably be best seen rather than read. I think an audio version is quite an acceptable substitute though, far better than reading it as text. There is no need for scenery &amp; the actors' voices can speak well enough for themselves. The meat of this play is in the overall idea, so even poor translations can't ruin it. I think a good translation or reading our discussion to catch some of the nuances is well worth it, though. Capek was kind of a genius.<br /><br />Highly recommended to one &amp; all, but it's a must for anyone interested in the evolution of SF.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6741165033642155717" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_74955656" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74955656" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1711167-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1226559547p2/1711167.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/74955656?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/1711167-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182087" href="/book/show/816443.R_U_R_">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer74955656" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9419895630913217887">People who pick this up to read probably know already that this is the first time that the word 'robots' was ever used, and that's only interesting, I think, because all of the thematic explorations found in robot literature and art such as in Asimov and Kubrick and all those little 'Terminator' movies were already there in the play! There's the 'what does it mean to be human', the war on the humans, religious implications (did mankind kill god?), all that stuff. There was an interesting forward</span>
  <span id="freeText9419895630913217887" style="display:none">People who pick this up to read probably know already that this is the first time that the word 'robots' was ever used, and that's only interesting, I think, because all of the thematic explorations found in robot literature and art such as in Asimov and Kubrick and all those little 'Terminator' movies were already there in the play! There's the 'what does it mean to be human', the war on the humans, religious implications (did mankind kill god?), all that stuff. There was an interesting forward to the book of Capek's other books, 'War With the Newts', and it talks about the invention of the word 'Robot' and how the dictionary can trace the origen to a specific date, Oct. 9, 1922 the premiere date, this one of only a couple of words, similar to okay, that could be uttered in any city and be understood.<br /><br />I forget sometimes how much I like reading plays. Just as much as anything else really, but I think of plays and how stark they are of surroundings and how silly the dialogue often is ("Oh darling you simply must marry one of us for we all love you very much, blah, blah, blah") and how quickly everything climaxes and descends and -all the sudden everybody's in love! All that stuff, so I look at plays on the shelf and kind of snear. But then I actually pick one up and read it, and they're always a lot of fun to read!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9419895630913217887" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1956645412" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1956645412" />
    <a title="Arun Divakar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2747498-arun-divakar"><img alt="Arun Divakar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1253201408p2/2747498.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1956645412?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Arun Divakar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Arun Divakar" href="/user/show/2747498-arun-divakar">Arun Divakar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/21048123-r-u-r">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1956645412" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9866117808632634893">Popular culture makes us think of Robots as entities that achieve a near mythical status over time and thereby replacing humans in the natural order of things. In a multitude narratives across books and movies we see them rising up in rebellion over humans and being superior beings they pulverize human resistance in no time. This plot has now be rehashed so many times that it is a cliché and yet at a very early stage in the life of sci-fi this might have been an amazingly fresh idea. This little</span>
  <span id="freeText9866117808632634893" style="display:none">Popular culture makes us think of Robots as entities that achieve a near mythical status over time and thereby replacing humans in the natural order of things. In a multitude narratives across books and movies we see them rising up in rebellion over humans and being superior beings they pulverize human resistance in no time. This plot has now be rehashed so many times that it is a cliché and yet at a very early stage in the life of sci-fi this might have been an amazingly fresh idea. This little book was the germ of that idea and the one that came up with the name ‘Robot’ and interestingly for a book which is an account of a robot rebellion, the story is extremely humane.<br /><br />Ever considered this ? <br /><br /><b>" From a technical point of view, the whole of childhood is quite pointless. Simply a waste of time."</b> <br /><br />Childhood is that carefree phase when almost all is fun and games and nostalgia and yet speaking from a productivity standpoint, one can always say that it is a zero sum game. Now to dispel any thoughts about the relevance of this quote, this is not aimed at a human being but at a robot. When a humanoid robot is manufactured, it is almost always a fully grown adult specimen for the basic reason that it can be productive from the time it leaves the assembly hall. The fundamental principle that the designers at Rossum’s Universal Robots (RUR) relies on is productivity. They have an almost Kaizen like approach to robot production which they extoll at the drop of a hat. They avoid anything that is not to do with effectiveness and focus only on making their machines the most humanoid paragons of efficiency. Interestingly they also realize the folly of trying to replicate a human being in a robot for they realize that the complexity of a human body is unique and does not lend itself to reproduction easily. All these high opinions were bound to fall from grace which they eventually do. The designers in their folly create robots who resemble each other exactly with the end result being that for them forming a body of resistance becomes much easier. After the robots turn the tables on us and humanity as represented by the designers at RUR are under siege, they hatch yet another plan. This is to henceforth design robots across different nations with varying colors, creeds and nationalities and thereby avoiding such a congregation ever again : rings a bell ?<br /><br />The core of RUR is not sci-fi but it is an examination of human folly. It is a story of egos crashing down, of the snubbing of arrogance, of the weakness of people and the pettiness we carry in our hearts. The Robots just happen to be there to edge things slightly over the cliff but essentially the characters themselves jump over the precipice all by themselves.<br /><br />Recommended. A very short but memorable read.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9866117808632634893" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2167933804" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2167933804" />
    <a title="Khashayar Mohammadi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32374363-khashayar-mohammadi"><img alt="Khashayar Mohammadi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493878334p2/32374363.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2167933804?book_show_action=true">Oct 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Khashayar Mohammadi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Khashayar Mohammadi" href="/user/show/32374363-khashayar-mohammadi">Khashayar Mohammadi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671466053" href="/book/show/3034409-r-u-r">review of another edition</a>

<div id="review_2389849073" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2389849073" />
    <a title="Ed Erwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1411444-ed-erwin"><img alt="Ed Erwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604431321p2/1411444.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2389849073?book_show_action=true">May 12, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ed Erwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ed Erwin" href="/user/show/1411444-ed-erwin">Ed Erwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781557422552" href="/book/show/24537100-r-u-r">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1411444-ed-erwin?shelf=sf">sf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1411444-ed-erwin?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2389849073" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14353753604199573272">You all know this is the play that introduced the word "Robot". But is this 100-year-old play worth reading? I think so. You probably won't find many ideas here you haven't seen before. But you might be surprised by how many of the modern tropes were already there this early.<br/><br/>So, if you don't read Czech language, which version should you use? They are considerably different.<br/><br/>The most common version in English is by Paul Selver (1922) then modified by Nigel Playfair (1923)<br/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://preprints.readingroo.ms/RUR/rur.pdf'>http://preprints.readingro</a></span>
  <span id="freeText14353753604199573272" style="display:none">You all know this is the play that introduced the word "Robot". But is this 100-year-old play worth reading? I think so. You probably won't find many ideas here you haven't seen before. But you might be surprised by how many of the modern tropes were already there this early.<br /><br />So, if you don't read Czech language, which version should you use? They are considerably different.<br /><br />The most common version in English is by Paul Selver (1922) then modified by Nigel Playfair (1923)<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://preprints.readingroo.ms/RUR/rur.pdf">http://preprints.readingroo.ms/RUR/ru...</a><br /><br />This is the version that is most likely to be seen on stage. But it is very much modified from the original. It is shorter, removes some characters, and divides the acts differently. (Original has Prologue and 3 acts. Modified has 3 acts plus an Epilogue.) I do not recommend reading this version because of the modifications. If you are performing it, though, this might be a good choice since the changes were probably intended to make performance easier.<br /><br />The first full-length version in English is by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3287107.Claudia_Novack_Jones" title="Claudia Novack-Jones" rel="nofollow noopener">Claudia Novack-Jones</a> (1989) ISBN 13: 9780141182087 <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/816443.R_U_R___Rossum_s_Universal_Robots_" title="R.U.R. (Rossum's Universal Robots) by Karel Čapek" rel="nofollow noopener">R.U.R.</a>. It stays very close to the structure of the original, as can be seen in the dual-language version here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.czech-language.cz/translations/rur-introen.html">http://www.czech-language.cz/translat...</a><br /><br />Peter Majer and Cathy Porter did another full-length version in 1999. I haven't read it, but find it odd that they changed the title to "Reason's Universal Robots". This version is available in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18924101.Capek_Four_Plays_R__U__R___The_Insect_Play__The_Makropulos_Case__The_White_Plague" title="Capek Four Plays R. U. R.; The Insect Play; The Makropulos Case; The White Plague by Karel Čapek" rel="nofollow noopener">Capek Four Plays</a><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/205263.David_Wylie" title="David Wylie" rel="nofollow noopener">David Wylie</a> did another version in 2006. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24537100.R_U_R___Rossum_s_Universal_Robots_" title="R.U.R. (Rossum's Universal Robots) by Karel Čapek" rel="nofollow noopener">R.U.R.</a>. It can be downloaded for free from the goodreads page, or from <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://ebooks.adelaide.edu.au/c/capek/karel/rur/">https://ebooks.adelaide.edu.au/c/cape...</a><br /><br />I read the version by David Wylie and it was fine. It has the full text and keeps the language very plain and simple. The character of Nana is given an interesting lower-class British speech pattern: "She doe'n'alf get some funny ideas!" <br /><br />I wish I had read the version by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3287107.Claudia_Novack_Jones" title="Claudia Novack-Jones" rel="nofollow noopener">Claudia Novack-Jones</a> . It is much more poetic. <br /><br />Compare the straightforward David Wylie: "You're still on watch, you star of man, steady glow and perfect flame, bright clear sprite of man's invention. Every beam brings thought and greatness... Torch that passes hand to hand, age to age, and ever onward. — That evening lamp in the family home. — Children, children now it's time for sleep. (Lamp goes out.)"<br /><br />To the poetic Claudia Novack-Jones: "You still stand watch, O human star, burning without a flicker, perfect flame, bright and resourceful spirit. Each of your rays a great idea — O torch which passes from hand to hand, from age to age, world without end. — Eternal lamp of the family. Children, children, it's time to go to bed. (The lamp goes out.)"<br /><br />To the drastically trimmed Selver/Playfair: "Man's power! May it keep watch over us. — Man's power. — Yes! A torch to be given from hand to hand, from age to age, forever! (The lamp goes out.)"<br /><br />Original: "Ještě bdíš, lidská hvězdo, záříš bez kmitu, dokonalý plameni, duchu jasný a vynalézavý. Každý tvůj paprsek je veliká myšlenka — Pochodeň, která koluje z ruky do ruky, z věku do věku, věčně dál. — Večerní lampa rodiny. Děti, děti, musíte už spat. (Žárovka zhasne.)"<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14353753604199573272" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2378999603" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2378999603" />
    <a title="Peter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32887118-peter"><img alt="Peter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1404796244p2/32887118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2378999603?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Peter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Peter" href="/user/show/32887118-peter">Peter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/34228469-r-u-r">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32887118-peter?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32887118-peter?shelf=sf-fantasy">sf-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32887118-peter?shelf=drama">drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2378999603" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9083136825181648049">This book foretells many of the dangers and desasters of modern life, including ecological destruction, artificial intelligence, autonomous devices. And it demonstrates the importance of technology impact assessment. Here robots are living creatures from the onset and the way they are treated reminds of slavery and serfdom.<br/><br/>In contrast to the accelerated development of technology the human mind (or 'rozum' in Czech language) has not evolved much beyond premordial times. The motivation for men's a</span>
  <span id="freeText9083136825181648049" style="display:none">This book foretells many of the dangers and desasters of modern life, including ecological destruction, artificial intelligence, autonomous devices. And it demonstrates the importance of technology impact assessment. Here robots are living creatures from the onset and the way they are treated reminds of slavery and serfdom.<br /><br />In contrast to the accelerated development of technology the human mind (or 'rozum' in Czech language) has not evolved much beyond premordial times. The motivation for men's actions still comes from strive for power, wealth or women (men in this case has to be understood as males - the females in this drama are limited to their role as loving but stupid companion or Pandora [both Helena] or wailing observers [Nana]).<br /><br />Being confined to the R.U.R. factory island we observe the decline of the world and humanity through the eyes and ears of the company's directors. They each represent a certain type of character - the fighter, the complacent, the sceptic, the materialist, the doer - similar to masks in an ancient Greek drama, while the women play the role of the chorus. The reference to antiquity is underlined by the choice of Roman names for the robots<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[, most imporantantly 'Primus', who becomes the forefather of the new robot race. Life endures in the end, but is this a soothing fact when mankind vanished? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. <br /><br />I am glad that I finally read this drama, which coined the term 'robot'. But though I admire Čapek's foresight, he did not manage to fully embrace the problems of artificial life and its repercussions on mankind. In that respect I am much more with Isaac Asimov and his robot stories.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9083136825181648049" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Maggie ☘" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/40477684-maggie"><img alt="Maggie ☘" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1591529603p2/40477684.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3338604135?book_show_action=true">Sep 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Maggie ☘" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Maggie ☘" href="/user/show/40477684-maggie">Maggie ☘</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/40477684-maggie?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3338604135" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17651438027111387060">Many already know that it was in this short play R.U.R. that the word Robot first appeared. I wanted to get into the author's books and this work gave me the opportunity to try with a shorter work first, before trying for War with the Newts. I was also really interested to read the novel where Čapek invented the than new word as well as see the sci-fi elements - very slight elements, while ahead of its time in theory and thought, it's still a novel of it's time (for example I would've expected t</span>
  <span id="freeText17651438027111387060" style="display:none">Many already know that it was in this short play R.U.R. that the word Robot first appeared. I wanted to get into the author's books and this work gave me the opportunity to try with a shorter work first, before trying for War with the Newts. I was also really interested to read the novel where Čapek invented the than new word as well as see the sci-fi elements - very slight elements, while ahead of its time in theory and thought, it's still a novel of it's time (for example I would've expected the author to give a better role - or really, at least one that is consistant and makes sense from start to finish - to Helena in a modern play). It was very easy play to read (in one or two sittings) and quite short. It's definitely not the last work I'm going to read by the author. Čapek was, in many ways, ahead of his time and there were a lot of interesting thoughts on society, capitalism, the chase for money, for cheaper products in shortest time, social classes etc. 3.5</span>
  <a data-text-id="17651438027111387060" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1388826172" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1388826172" />
    <a title="sologdin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin"><img alt="sologdin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1307928658p2/5610133.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1388826172?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="sologdin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="sologdin" href="/user/show/5610133-sologdin">sologdin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=blew-it-up-you-maniacs">blew-it-up-you-maniacs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5610133-sologdin?shelf=burns-like-cold-iron">burns-like-cold-iron</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1388826172" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13722173482233746092">Probably important as the prototype for the basic robot narrative, which is as follows:<br/><br/>a)	Humans manufacture slave laborers whose own needs are minimal in order to lower costs and break unions;<br/><br/>b)	Humans equip slave laborers with skills sufficient to carry out productive tasks, including heavy industrial, technical, academic, and military functions;<br/><br/>c)	Humans construct slave laborers who lack any desires of their own and are accordingly not market participants and therefore require no wages;<br/><br/>d)	Hum</span>
  <span id="freeText13722173482233746092" style="display:none">Probably important as the prototype for the basic robot narrative, which is as follows:<br /><br />a)	Humans manufacture slave laborers whose own needs are minimal in order to lower costs and break unions;<br /><br />b)	Humans equip slave laborers with skills sufficient to carry out productive tasks, including heavy industrial, technical, academic, and military functions;<br /><br />c)	Humans construct slave laborers who lack any desires of their own and are accordingly not market participants and therefore require no wages;<br /><br />d)	Humans are overthrown by slave population, which, having come somehow to self-consciousness as a Hegelian <i>klasse fur sich</i>, has superseded owners in all relevant functions and abilities;<br /><br />e)	Humans are subject to genocide during the course or because of slave uprising, which genocide is characterized as self-defense/military necessity on the one hand or industrial necessity on the other (rather than as justice/revenge).<br /><br />The text here is mostly the conversations of non-robot slavers, chronicling the rise and fall of the robot industry.<br /><br />There’d already been quite a bit of water under the bridge as concerns robot fictions, going back to Baum in the Oz books, and long before in Spenser’s <i>Faerie Queene</i> with the ‘snowy’ or ‘false’ Florimell, created through witchcraft (and thereby embodying the trite figures of Petrarchan sonnets). <br /><br />Prior to that, we have automata of sorts in Greek mythology: <blockquote> Now instruments are of various sorts; some are living, others lifeless; in the rudder, the pilot of a ship has a lifeless, in the look-out man, a living instrument; for in the arts the servant is a kind of instrument. Thus, too, a possession is an instrument for maintaining life. And so, in the arrangement of the family, a slave is a living possession, and property a number of such instruments; and the servant is himself an instrument which takes precedence of all other instruments. For if every instrument could accomplish its own work, obeying or anticipating the will of others, like the statues of Daedalus, or the tripods of Hephaestus, which, says the poet, "of their own accord entered the assembly of the Gods;" if, in like manner, the shuttle would weave and the plectrum touch the lyre without a hand to guide them, chief workmen would not want servants, nor masters slaves. </blockquote>(Aristotle’s <i>Politics</i>, at I.4, which must be refracted through VII.10: “The very best thing of all would be that the husbandmen should be slaves taken from among men who are not all of the same race and not spirited, for if they have no spirit they will be better suited for their work, and there will be no danger of their making a revolution.”) There’s also Talos, bronze guardian of Crete, defeated by Medea during the voyage of the Argo, with molten lead for blood. See Apollonius’ <i>Argonautica</i> at IV.1638, Apollodorus’ <i>Library</i>, at I.140). <br /><br />I’m not sure if all instances prior to Capek involve manufactured persons created for the purposes of reducing labor costs (though the Slavic root of <i>robot</i> signifies ‘forced labor’), resulting in the inexorable rebellion of the slave laborers and probable consequent destruction of the slave owners (not simply their Marxist abolition as a ruling class, but their physical extermination (as in <i>The Terminator</i>, <i>The Matrix</i>, <i>Battlestar Galactica</i>), but this particular line exhausts itself conceptually here, becoming cliché thereafter—which is why the next step in robot stories is Asimov a generation later writing about robots (in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/41804.I__Robot__Robot___0_1_" title="I, Robot (Robot, #0.1) by Isaac Asimov" rel="nofollow noopener"> I, Robot</a>) who are incapable of revolution, via three laws of robotics. That this repressed returns via the spectre of robot management of human society (in the Zeroth Law, as first implied by Asimov’s “The Evitable Conflict” (op. cit.)), should be taken as confirmation that the basic narrative initiated by Capek remains in force despite Asimov’s specific intent to render slave labor immune to revolution. (We have all this occurring not as tragedy, but as farce, in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9634967.Robopocalypse__Robopocalypse___1_" title="Robopocalypse (Robopocalypse, #1) by Daniel H. Wilson" rel="nofollow noopener"> Robopocalypse</a>.) In Asimov, of course, robot management of human society is almost always presented as non-genocidal and benign, for the betterment of humanity, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[though the most glaring exception is how robots nuke Earth in <i>Robots and Empire</i>, which is still, however, compliant with the Zeroth <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />Of course these texts are not just about robots, just as zombie narratives are not about the undead; the latter body of stories is related intimately to guaranteeing a stable supply of low cost labor, normally through necromancy or bodily possession via sortilege. In both sets of narratives, the point is that the human population must subdue its wayward labor force; the structure of the narrative (human protagonist perspectives, tragic form, indicia of suspense, &amp;c.) forces the receiver to sympathize with the proprietors and to regard what is essentially a slave uprising with horror (mostly because philistine writers offer genocide as the only alternative to robot slavery—strikingly similar to how rightists such as Herb Spencer and Mags Thatcher intoned that there is no alternative to capitalism). Thoughtful readers should accordingly be wary of any robot narrative that presents a slave society with the necessity of a genocidal slave uprising, as though it were unthinkable to emancipate those wrongly held in servitude and restructure society on an egalitarian basis.<br />	<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13722173482233746092" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer351830834128888362">"There will be no poverty. All work will be done by living machines. Everybody will be free from worry and liberated from the degradation of labor. Everybody will live only to perfect himself."<br/><br/>▫️From R.U.R. (Rossum's Universal Robots) by Karel Čapek, translated from the Czech by Paul Selver and Nigel Playfair<br/><br/>A manufacturing company engineers humanoid machines to take over all manual labor, progressing humans towards a utopian society...   <span id="freeText351830834128888362" style="display:none">"There will be no poverty. All work will be done by living machines. Everybody will be free from worry and liberated from the degradation of labor. Everybody will live only to perfect himself."<br /><br />▫️From R.U.R. (Rossum's Universal Robots) by Karel Čapek, translated from the Czech by Paul Selver and Nigel Playfair<br /><br />A manufacturing company engineers humanoid machines to take over all manual labor, progressing humans towards a utopian society... That is the plan, of course, until a small change in the "recipe" that brings sentience and desire to these machines. Creations striking back at their creators; drastic and deadly consequences for all humanity!<br /><br />R.U.R. is a melodramatic Czech #scifi for the stage. Čapek's play, written in 1920, coined the English word ROBOT, from a Czech dialect term for 'drudgery'. This play is only 60-some pages, but a conceptual drama that is very readable and entertaining, and will surprise you with its relevance to today.<br /><br />The robots of R.U.R. actually take a biological form, covered in skin, instead of the vintage automaton that often comes to mind. The cast and setting are small with three short acts, but large scale in scope, with a surprise ending in the epilogue.<br /><br />A robopocalypse, writ for the interwar period. What's amazing is how philosophical and prescient this is 99 years after it's publication and debut. We are just now seeing some of these predictions become reality.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10445977165962118948">This short play is primarily of historical interest, since the story is (now) very familiar and the characterization is quite meager. The play is additionally hampered by a poor translation. I read two different editions of the play, both crediting the same translator, but found stark differences between them. The version pictured here seems more complete, but I found several passages truncated or entirely removed from the Dover Thrift Edition (go figure).<br/><br/>If you are at all interested in the hist</span>
  <span id="freeText10445977165962118948" style="display:none">This short play is primarily of historical interest, since the story is (now) very familiar and the characterization is quite meager. The play is additionally hampered by a poor translation. I read two different editions of the play, both crediting the same translator, but found stark differences between them. The version pictured here seems more complete, but I found several passages truncated or entirely removed from the Dover Thrift Edition (go figure).<br /><br />If you are at all interested in the history of robot stories, this is a must read for its depiction of a robot revolution, but if you are just looking for casual entertainment or--the Maker forbid--a script to produce, go elsewhere. Feminist scholars may find the portrayal of the lone female character enlightening (or horrifying), and fans of crazy scientists will see some early versions of that stereotype, but this story has now been told in more creative and engaging ways by other authors. Still, all respect to Capek for giving us the terminology for an entire genre.</span>

<div id="review_1429954519" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1429954519" />
    <a title="Nicky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/223837-nicky"><img alt="Nicky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308224598p2/223837.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1429954519?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nicky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nicky" href="/user/show/223837-nicky">Nicky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=speculative-fiction">speculative-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/223837-nicky?shelf=plays">plays</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1429954519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15826052405786888248">When you read about robots, you have Capek to thank. He’s the one who first came up with that word for a separate group of manufactured people (automatons?) who carry out work around the house, in factories and in the fields, subservient to the needs of humans. He’s the first one who posited how they might rise up, and declare themselves as people too. It’s a shortish play, and one which I don’t always quite get — what’s with the men all falling in love with the one female character? Why do the </span>
  <span id="freeText15826052405786888248" style="display:none">When you read about robots, you have Capek to thank. He’s the one who first came up with that word for a separate group of manufactured people (automatons?) who carry out work around the house, in factories and in the fields, subservient to the needs of humans. He’s the first one who posited how they might rise up, and declare themselves as people too. It’s a shortish play, and one which I don’t always quite get — what’s with the men all falling in love with the one female character? Why do the characters act the way they do?<br /><br />But it is fascinating to come back to one of the places where it all started, and it was worth the read to see where some of the themes and preoccupations of later science fiction originated.<br /><br /><a href="https://breathesbooks.com/2018/01/29/review-rur/" rel="nofollow noopener">Reviewed for The Bibliophibian.</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="15826052405786888248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2431854182" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2431854182" />
    <a title="Noah Eigenfeld" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21853130-noah-eigenfeld"><img alt="Noah Eigenfeld" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575490851p2/21853130.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2431854182?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Noah Eigenfeld" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Noah Eigenfeld" href="/user/show/21853130-noah-eigenfeld">Noah Eigenfeld</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182087" href="/book/show/816443.R_U_R_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/21853130-noah-eigenfeld?shelf=finished-in-2019">finished-in-2019</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2431854182" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2352934325339553163">Moderately interesting, but only really worth reading now for curiosity’s sake.<br/><br/>This play’s claim to fame is the invention of the word “robot.” While a fun fact, I’m not sure that there’s much else of significance in the play itself. It’s a robot uprising story, sure, but the ideas at hand are not necessarily new, and have been explored in more interesting ways since this play was written. What’s more of a stumbling block is the way the plot unfolds offstage and the lacking characters. Most of th</span>
  <span id="freeText2352934325339553163" style="display:none">Moderately interesting, but only really worth reading now for curiosity’s sake.<br /><br />This play’s claim to fame is the invention of the word “robot.” While a fun fact, I’m not sure that there’s much else of significance in the play itself. It’s a robot uprising story, sure, but the ideas at hand are not necessarily new, and have been explored in more interesting ways since this play was written. What’s more of a stumbling block is the way the plot unfolds offstage and the lacking characters. Most of the moral quandaries exist merely in dialogue between one bland professor-type and another bland professor-type. At a certain point, I stopped caring about the names delivering lines, so long as they weren’t either the female or male lead. It did not impact the reading experience.<br /><br />Speaking of our leads, I enjoyed the amount of agency given to the female lead, but I also think it’s problematic that her actions may or may not have doomed humanity. I think there’s room for interpretation. A charitable performance could emphasize that she’s taking action, while the rest of our male characters are lounging around in self-assurance; an uncharitable performance could drive home an Eve/Pandora-style woman-is-the-cause-of-all-evil-and-chaos approach. The lightning-fast “okay, you’ll do” romance in the first scene does not help her case.<br /><br />The characters who really get short-changed in the play are the robots themselves. They are present, do stuff offstage, and two randos pop onstage last-minute to prove the author’s point. I can see the seeds of more interesting stories here (Do Androids Dream of Electric Sheep, for example, expands on the dilemma of robots trying to extend their lifespan; see Frankenstein for manmade creatures who want to procreate, etc.), but they aren’t given time to grow.<br /><br />Overall, this play didn’t have much sticking power, but might be useful in bar trivia someday.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2352934325339553163" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

           <span class="uitext reviewText">Go to it, if you hear of a theatrical production in your city that has garnered good reviews,</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Czarny by: </span>
      Dominik Hasek
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=czech-lit">czech-lit</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1079235226" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4451619699358495376">Theatre directors must absolutely love R.U.R. that licenses them to do absolutely anything with it. R.U.R. which is about everything also belongs to all known twentieth century literary movements: theatre de l'absurde, Dadaism, surrealism, symbolism and modernism. I agree with the critic who described it as the great masterpiece of the Czech avant-garde. <br/>The basic plot line is simple. A mad scientist invents a Robot which is more like Mary Shelley's Frankenstein in that it is built with plasma t</span>
  <span id="freeText4451619699358495376" style="display:none">Theatre directors must absolutely love R.U.R. that licenses them to do absolutely anything with it. R.U.R. which is about everything also belongs to all known twentieth century literary movements: theatre de l'absurde, Dadaism, surrealism, symbolism and modernism. I agree with the critic who described it as the great masterpiece of the Czech avant-garde. <br />The basic plot line is simple. A mad scientist invents a Robot which is more like Mary Shelley's Frankenstein in that it is built with plasma than it is like C-3PO or R2-D2 from Star Wars which are metallic. The mad scientist forms a company R.U.R. (Rosumovi Univerzální Roboti) that mass produces the robots.<br />The robots take over all of man's work and thus put an end to human drudgery. At this point women lose the ability to bear children as is explained by the following passage which appears in Act Two:<br /><br />Helen the Heroine: Why aren't women having babies?<br />A bad guy: Because it is no longer necessary. We're already living in Paradise. Because human labour is no longer necessary. Because pain isn't necessary. Because people no longer need to do anything but consume.<br /><br />The human race proceeds to die out. The robots rebel and take over. However, being good machines they start to die as soon as the warranty expires. Because prior to her death Helen the Heroine has destroyed the formula for making the robots which dooms the robot race. Fortunately a pair of original human beings are discovered so that the whole process can begin all over again.<br />This play reads like the worst of pulp science fiction but in the hands of a talented director it can perform any task. It can be become a critique of the consumer society, show how workers (or Robotnik in Czech) are exploited by the capitalist society, illustrate man's hubris when he believes that he can create life like God, display the magnitude of the Nemesis that follows, plead for the necessity of human suffering or do just about anything else.<br /><br />I had the misfortune to attend a theatrical performance that presented the robots as alienated proletarians. You are likely to be luckier however. Given the performance history of this work, it is quite certain that you will see something different.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="4451619699358495376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8677760790703759866"><blockquote><i>It was a great thing to be a man. There was something immense about it.</i> (47)</blockquote>These words are uttered in the face of a robot uprising and imminent takeover, when all seems lost for human beings. I am not a big science fiction fan, but <i>R. U. R.</i> had intrigued me for some time. Written in 1920, it introduced and popularized the word ‘robot’ (the term derives from a Czech dialect word for ‘drudgery’ – it was suggested to Karel by his older brother Joseph). An early classic of artificial intelligence, </span>
  <span id="freeText8677760790703759866" style="display:none"><blockquote><i>It was a great thing to be a man. There was something immense about it.</i> (47)</blockquote>These words are uttered in the face of a robot uprising and imminent takeover, when all seems lost for human beings. I am not a big science fiction fan, but <i>R. U. R.</i> had intrigued me for some time. Written in 1920, it introduced and popularized the word ‘robot’ (the term derives from a Czech dialect word for ‘drudgery’ – it was suggested to Karel by his older brother Joseph). An early classic of artificial intelligence, the play looks to a future in which all workers are automatons.<br /><br />Reading <i>R. U. R.</i> (Rossum’s Universal Robots) was like reading the generic script to all subsequent robot/artificial intelligence stories, whether in film or literature; I doubt that its influence on these can be overstated. I am not sure that the medium that Čapek chose – a play – allowed for adequate treatment of the topic. With so much to be said and worked out, some aspects of characters and plot felt significantly underdeveloped. A novella, for instance, might have allowed for more justice to be done to the abundance and richness of ideas surrounding the subject.<br /><br />Having said that, the play was highly readable, engaging, and its theme still fascinates. It is a humane work interspersed with some sharp satire. This had me laughing out loud:<blockquote><i>My dear Miss Glory, we’ve had close upon a hundred saviors and prophets here. Every ship brings us some. Missionaries, anarchists, Salvation Army, all sorts. It’s astonishing what a number of churches and idiots there are in the world.</i> (12)</blockquote><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8677760790703759866" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
