<span id="freeTextContainer4190604879819048399">I can't believe I'd never read this before. I love Robots, I love robot revolts and the end of man. This is the book where we get the word robot from and I'd still not gotten around to reading it! But I am SOOOO glad I did. It was brilliant. Everything that modern scifi has been trying to say about robots since is all here!!! It's the perfect continuation on from Frankenstein. It's depressing and sees the destruction of the human race, yet it has a happy ending. I would love to see it performed </span>
  <span id="freeText4190604879819048399" style="display:none">I can't believe I'd never read this before. I love Robots, I love robot revolts and the end of man. This is the book where we get the word robot from and I'd still not gotten around to reading it! But I am SOOOO glad I did. It was brilliant. Everything that modern scifi has been trying to say about robots since is all here!!! It's the perfect continuation on from Frankenstein. It's depressing and sees the destruction of the human race, yet it has a happy ending. I would love to see it performed as a play. I loved the fact that the robots were perfectly humanoid in appearance (and to be ultimately ironic wouldn't be classed as robots in today's scifi terms). That they were originally invented to save labour but ended up being used by nationalist governments to fight war. I liked that there was a woman fighting for robot rights and trying to make them more human. There were a few gender issues but overall I found it throughly enjoyable. It really should be read by everyone who likes robots. It's everything that Cameron and BSG stole and re-packaged and honestly I think the original does it much better.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4190604879819048399" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9631604186076335045">I don't read that many plays, but I should probably read more considering that I work in theatre. I picked this one up primarily because it's famous for coining the term "robot". The creatures in Čapek's work aren't really what we typically consider robots today, though--they're more biological than mechanical. <br/><br/>Written in 1920 and first performed in 1921, this was way ahead of its time.   <span id="freeText9631604186076335045" style="display:none">I don't read that many plays, but I should probably read more considering that I work in theatre. I picked this one up primarily because it's famous for coining the term "robot". The creatures in Čapek's work aren't really what we typically consider robots today, though--they're more biological than mechanical. <br /><br />Written in 1920 and first performed in 1921, this was way ahead of its time. The machines-rebelling-against-their-masters trope is ubiquitous in contemporary science fiction, and <i>R.U.R.</i> is arguably where it all started. It's a dark and apocalyptic vision of the future, but it's also a social commentary and witty satire. There are quite a few bits that are very funny. I wanted to read this for the historical contribution it has made, but it truly stands up today and I enjoyed it based on its own merit. <br /><br />I'd love to be involved in a production of this.</span>

<div id="review_2991002951" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2991002951" />
    <a title="Andrei Vasilachi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58499571-andrei-vasilachi"><img alt="Andrei Vasilachi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470880465p2/58499571.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2991002951?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrei Vasilachi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrei Vasilachi" href="/user/show/58499571-andrei-vasilachi">Andrei Vasilachi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182087" href="/book/show/816443.R_U_R_">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2991002951&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2991002951" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16565598712859634867"><b>4/5</b><br/><br/>The drama that started it all, at least when it comes to “robots”—this theatre piece with three acts and a prologue popularized the term “robot” in sci-fi literature. It was written by one of the greatest Czech writers you probably never heard of—Karel Čapek. The story has become almost a cliché: A visionary man wants to “scientifically dethrone God, and prove that God is unnecessary (p. 7)”, so he invents robots (resembling men) and builds them to serve our needs—mostly manual labor and fact</span>
  <span id="freeText16565598712859634867" style="display:none"><b>4/5</b><br /><br />The drama that started it all, at least when it comes to “robots”—this theatre piece with three acts and a prologue popularized the term “robot” in sci-fi literature. It was written by one of the greatest Czech writers you probably never heard of—Karel Čapek. The story has become almost a cliché: A visionary man wants to “scientifically dethrone God, and prove that God is unnecessary (p. 7)”, so he invents robots (resembling men) and builds them to serve our needs—mostly manual labor and factory work, but then it extends to them being personal assistants and doing any routine tasks that people don’t want to do. At first, robots are pretty basic, they’re not conscious and just serve us, but then shit starts to happen. Sound familiar? <br /><br />Some humans oppose the “oppression”, moralizing the issue and arguing that robots deserve to have rights; some think robots are a means to an end—they don’t feel anything, can work endlessly without complaining, are not conscious and thus are not able to suffer; some want all men to become “masters” and to get rid of the social hierarchy; others think that life loses meaning if people don’t work, blaming the utopian ideal of endless leisure. Then some human scientist decides to “play God” by improving the robots and making them more intelligent and “more human”. Robots become conscious, realize they’re smarter than humans, form a rebellion, and wipe the humans out. The end. (the actual end is a bit more nuanced, but I won’t spoil it).<br /><br />I Robot? Terminator? Matrix? Westworld? You name it. All these were (directly or loosely) inspired by Capek’s work. That’s why it’s fascinating to read (or attend a play of) R.U.R.—it’s original and timeless. It’s deceivingly simple and succinct, yet it’s profoundly archetypal—you can find all the different philosophies on the “robot issue” portrayed via the characters of this play.<br /><br />Having been written in 1920, the setting is clear: it’s the interwar period, there’s lots of factories courtesy of the industrial revolution (which started a century ago) and Europe needs to be rebuilt after the war. Hence the relentless race for more efficiency was the uber-ideology of the day. The main fear and worry portrayed in literature and in society at the time was that it dehumanized people, making them means to an end, the end being open-ended: more profit, more production, more workers, more manual labor, more profit, etc. <br /><br />The word “robot” wasn’t actually invented by Čapek, it comes from the slavic “robota” (pабота; work; forced laborer). It’s a term which classified those peasants obligated to compulsory service under the feudal system—a system which was eventually abolished by the “Robot Patent” issued by Joseph II, Holy Roman Emperor, in the 1700s, which abolished compulsory labor (robot) of working peasants; then it was restored again by Leopold II; then came the European Revolutions of 1848 which broke the last legal tie which held the peasants to the land, and was seen as a great victory by the peasants. So the story of robots’ rebellion is not accidental, it has real historical ties depicting the revolts of working peasants under feudal systems. <br /><br />Still, the modern term of “robot” as a “human automata” is first introduced in R.U.R.—and I should also note that Karel Čapek credited his brother Joseph for inventing this term.<br /><br />In the first half of the 20th century—when R.U.R. was written—there was an urgent need for more productivity, more need for work, more need for “robota”—for robots, you see. Taking this into context, it becomes clear what was Capek’s intention: R.U.R. is a dystopia and a satire, it seeks to bring to light the absurdity of our desires, our conflicting idealisms, our own hypocrisy and vanity. It seeks to provoke, not patronizingly, but empathetically. This, in my opinion, is where this story excels. It portrays characters with opposite philosophies and it makes us understand each one of them. No one is “totally right”, and no one is “totally wrong”. In other words: it’s complicated.<br /><br />Here’s Čapek himself describing this very same ambiguity:<br /><br /><i>“And now for my second idea, the comedy about truth. General director Domin tries to prove in the play that technical developments liberate man from heavy physical labor, and he is right. Alquist, the Tolstoyan architect, believes on the contrary that technical developments demoralize man, and I think that he is right, too. Busman thinks that only industrialism is capable of meeting modern needs; he is right. Helena instinctively fears all this human machinery, and she is quite right. And finally, the Robots themselves revolt against all these idealisms, and it seems that they are right as well.<br /><br />We don’t need to look for names for all these various antithetical idealisms. What I want to stress is that no matter whether these people are conservatives or socialists, yellow or red, all of them are right in a simple moral sense of the word. All of them have the most serious of motives, material and spiritual, for their beliefs, and according to their nature look for the greatest happiness for the greatest number of their fellows. I ask myself: isn’t it possible to see in the contemporary social conflicts taking place in the world an analogous struggle between two, three or five equally serious and noble idealisms? I believe that it is possible. The most dramatic element in modern civilization is the fact that one human truth stands against a truth no less human, one ideal against another ideal, one positive value against a value no less positive, and that the conflict does not represent, as we are often told, a struggle between a noble truth and vile, selfish evil.”</i><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16565598712859634867" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2285636860" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2285636860" />
    <a title="Greg" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6922605-greg"><img alt="Greg" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1320867217p2/6922605.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2285636860?book_show_action=true">Feb 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg" href="/user/show/6922605-greg">Greg</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182087" href="/book/show/816443.R_U_R_">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6922605-greg?shelf=drama">drama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2285636860" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3594997195627329928">In the introduction, Klima notes that you can detect relativistic philosophy in this play, that everyone has their own truth. Coining the term “robot,” Capek introduces a science fiction play all the way back in the 1920’s. Rossum, in the play, invents robots who will take on the role of work. Domin states that, “Those two quarreled brutally. The old atheist didn’t have a crumb of understanding for industry, and finally young Rossum shut him up in some laboratory where he could fiddle with his m</span>
  <span id="freeText3594997195627329928" style="display:none">In the introduction, Klima notes that you can detect relativistic philosophy in this play, that everyone has their own truth. Coining the term “robot,” Capek introduces a science fiction play all the way back in the 1920’s. Rossum, in the play, invents robots who will take on the role of work. Domin states that, “Those two quarreled brutally. The old atheist didn’t have a crumb of understanding for industry, and finally young Rossum shut him up in some laboratory where he could fiddle with his monumental abortions, and he himself undertook production from the standpoint of an engineer. Old Rossum literally cursed him, and before his death he bungled two more physiological monsters until he was finally found dead in his laboratory one day. That’s the whole story.” The truths of the creators of the robots are different. What will these robots do, and what will become of man? Domin later states, “but within the next ten years Rossum’s Universal Robots will produce so much wheat, so much cloth, so much everything that things will no longer have any value. Everyone will be able to take as much as he needs. There’ll be no more poverty. Yes, people will be out of work, but by then there’ll be no work left to be done. Everything will be done by living machines. People will do only what they enjoy. They will live only to perfect themselves.” <br /><br />This utopian idea is not without consequences. Helena and Alquist find themselves debating the future of mankind. In becoming superfluous, will they decide to bear children? Alquist comments to Domin, “nothing is stranger to man than his own image.”<br /><br />This far reaching play asks many questions, and finds troubling answers. Fantastic!<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/6922605-greg?page=1&amp;shelf=read" rel="nofollow noopener">See my other reviews here!</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3594997195627329928" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2285636860">

<div id="review_3108165324" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3108165324" />
    <a title="tortoise dreams" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51546630-tortoise-dreams"><img alt="tortoise dreams" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1456768521p2/51546630.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3108165324?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="tortoise dreams" class="user" itemprop="url" name="tortoise dreams" href="/user/show/51546630-tortoise-dreams">tortoise dreams</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780671466053" href="/book/show/3034409-r-u-r">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3108165324" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18093086172976876982"><i>Humanoid robots are created to ease the burden of work for humanity, but the story doesn't end there.</i><br/><br/><b>Play Review:</b> <i>R.U.R.</i> is parenthetically titled <i>Rossum's Universal Robots</i>, and this year will be a century old. Which means we need to place it in time to discuss it in context. In 1920 industrialization was king. The telephone, automobile, radio, and airplanes were new technology. Factories and the assembly line were also new, which led to a hellish vision of the future for workers. Were humans to</span>
  <span id="freeText18093086172976876982" style="display:none"><i>Humanoid robots are created to ease the burden of work for humanity, but the story doesn't end there.</i><br /><br /><b>Play Review:</b> <i>R.U.R.</i> is parenthetically titled <i>Rossum's Universal Robots</i>, and this year will be a century old. Which means we need to place it in time to discuss it in context. In 1920 industrialization was king. The telephone, automobile, radio, and airplanes were new technology. Factories and the assembly line were also new, which led to a hellish vision of the future for workers. Were humans to become robots mindlessly tending a conveyor belt? As the play (did you know it was a play? -- I didn't) was written a hundred years ago, Karel Capek (1890-1938) only had the benefit of 100 years of science fiction (I'm dating from Mary Shelley's <i>Frankenstein</i> (1818)) and not the second century of the genre we now know. As such, <i>R.U.R.</i> is superficially rather simple, exploring a variety of ideas quickly and in passing, allowing the reader to take the concepts raised in many directions. Concepts such as religion, free will, humanity ... . Capek's "robots" are not what we connote today -- they were more like androids or replicants (think <i>Blade Runner</i> or <i>Ex Machina</i>, capable of being mistaken for humans) than talking metal beings (such as C-3PO or anything by Isaac Asimov). But their purpose was the same. Rather than children in Thailand doing dangerous and mindless labor, these were emotionless creatures designed to uncomplainingly do tedious and difficult work. Instead of humans becoming robots, Capek imagined that we create actual robots to do the work. Prescient, ahead of its time, and written with enough humor to count as satire. [3½★]</span>
  <a data-text-id="18093086172976876982" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground">Readers also enjoyed</h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent"><div class='bookCarousel'>
<div class='carouselRow' style='width: 3600px'>
<ul>
<li class='cover' id='bookCover_4617242'>
<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4617242-krysa"><img alt="Krysař" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1295249629l/4617242._SX98_.jpg" />
</a>

  
<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('M6yNu3kA66nUoJqxIA8rJzq8eQ3z1ZjYkCD/kcrRJ/OFRtrkkmCA+s5EVYMOtfVsP8bhV+KC5NGxbBKd6LCRAg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

May, h<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText17648541333269066385\" style=\"display:none\">Often compared to Byron, Keats, Shelley, and Poe, called Lautreamont\'s &quot;elder brother&quot; by the Czech Surrealists, Karel Hynek Mácha (1810-1836) was the greatest Czech Romantic poet, and arguably the most influential of any poet in the language. May, his epic masterpiece, was published in April 1836, just seven months before his death. Considered the &quot;pearl&quot; of Czech poetry, it is a tale of seduction, revenge, and patricide. A paean as well to nature, the beauty of its music and its innovative use of language, expertly captured in this new translation by Marcela Sulak, has ensured the poem\'s lasting popularity. Scorned at first by the national revivalists of the 19th century for being &quot;un-Czech,&quot; Mácha was held up as a &quot;national&quot; poet by later generations, a fate which the interwar Czech avant-garde, who considered him a precursor, took it upon themselves to reverse.\n\nUnlike the other seminal 19th-century European poets, Mácha\'s work has been largely ignored in English translation. The present volume, the only available in English, provides the original Czech text in parallel and includes a series of illustrations by Jindřich Štyrský specifically created for the poem.<\/span>\n       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/439723.Karel_apek">About Karel Čapek</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/439723.Karel_apek"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1208185188p3/439723.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/439723.Karel_apek">Karel Čapek
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
614
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5938829600672835655">Karel Čapek is one of the the most influential Czech writers of the 20th century. He wrote with intelligence and humour on a wide variety of subjects. His works are known for their interesting and precise descriptions of reality, and Čapek is renowned for his excellent work with the Czech language. His play <i>R.U.R. (Rossum's Universal Robots)</i> first popularized the word "robot".</span>
